SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
In general, our field exploration concluded that the pavement structure at University 
Avenue, on average, consists of about 6.5 inches of Asphalt Concrete (AC) over 5 inches 
of base/subbase material. The subgrade soils generally consisted of dense sand and 
gravel and stiff clayey soils. Soft to medium hard basalt rock was encountered near Oahu 
Avenue. 
The pavement structure at Manoa Road, on average, consists of about 5 inches of 
AC over about 7.5 inches of base/subbase material. Where PCC was encountered below 
the AC surface, the average thickness of the PCC was about 7 inches. The subgrade soils 
generally consisted of stiff clayey soils.  
The pavement structure at East Manoa Road, on average, consists of 8 inches of 
AC over 6.5 inches of base/subbase material. Portland Cement Concrete (PCC) was 
encountered below the AC surface course between Manoa Road and Oahu Avenue. The 
average thickness of the PCC was about 4 inches. Clayey soils were generally 
encountered below the base/subbase material.  
We believe that the poor bonding of the AC overlays/lifts may have attributed to the 
distresses that developed in these roadways.  Our borings also indicated that the AC 
surface course might be underlain by PCC pavements at some areas of Manoa Road and 
East Manoa Road.  Existing cracks in the PCC could have reflected up to the AC surface 
course. 
Based on our observation of the existing pavements, we recommend reconstruction 
of these roadways with a new pavement section. Based on the traffic data provided and 
the subgrade conditions encountered, the new flexible pavement sections for the 
roadways under Rehabilitation of City Streets, Unit 81, should consist of the following:  
University Avenue (Coyne Street to Dole Street), Manoa Road (Nehoa Street to 
East Manoa Road), and East Manoa Road 
3.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete  
4.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete Base (92 Percent Max. Theoretical Specific Gravity) 
6.0-Inch Aggregate Subbase Course (95 Percent Relative Compaction) 
           13.0-Inch Total New Pavement Thickness 
SUMMARY OF FINDINGS AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
W.O. 6947-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page iv 
Hawaii • California 
University Avenue (Dole Street to Oahu Avenue) 
2.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete  
4.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete Base (92 Percent Max. Theoretical Specific Gravity) 
 6.0-Inch Aggregate Subbase Course (95 Percent Relative Compaction) 
 12.0-Inch Total New Pavement Thickness 
Manoa Road (East Manoa Road to Mauka End) 
2.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete  
3.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete Base (92 Percent Max. Theoretical Specific Gravity) 
 6.0-Inch Aggregate Subbase Course (95 Percent Relative Compaction) 
 11.0-Inch Total New Pavement Thickness 
The existing PCC pavement encountered at some areas of Manoa Road and East 
Manoa Road should be removed.  Leaving the existing PCC pavement and placing new 
AC over the PCC may result in existing cracks in the PCC pavement reflecting up to the 
new AC surface.   
If soft soils are encountered at the pavement subgrade level, we recommend 
providing a stabilization layer in lieu of vibratory proof-rolling the soft subgrade, which may 
induce a pumping condition.  The stabilization layer should consist of 8 inches of 
aggregate base course over a layer of geogrid (such as Tensar Triax TX 7 or equivalent) 
underlain by a woven geotextile fabric (such as Mirafi HP370 or equivalent). 
The text of this report should be referred to for detailed discussions and specific 
pavement design recommendations.

SECTION 1.  GENERAL 
1.1 
Introduction 
This report presents the results of our geotechnical engineering analyses 
performed for the Rehabilitation of City Streets, Unit 81 Portions of University Avenue, 
Manoa Road and East Manoa Road project in Honolulu on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. 
The project location and general vicinity are shown on the Project Location Map, 
Plate 1. 
This report summarizes the findings from our field exploration and laboratory 
testing, and presents our geotechnical engineering recommendations derived from our 
analyses for the roadway rehabilitation project. The findings and recommendations 
presented herein are subject to the limitations noted at the end of this report. 
1.2 
Project Considerations 
The Rehabilitation of City Streets, Unit 81 project is part of an overall Pavement 
Survey Project covering various locations across the City and County of Honolulu. 
Generally, the Unit 81 project will involve rehabilitation of approximately 1.1 miles of 
University Avenue, approximately 2.6 miles of Manoa Road, and approximately 
1.8 miles of East Manoa Road in Honolulu on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii.  These 
roadways consist of asphaltic concrete pavements. 
The limits of the pavement rehabilitation project along each roadway is presented 
in the following table.  In addition, the roadway alignments of the project are shown on 
the General Site Plan, Plate 2.

The purpose of our exploration was to obtain an overview of the surface and 
subsurface conditions to develop a soil/rock data set to formulate geotechnical 
engineering recommendations for the roadway rehabilitation project. Our work was

SECTION 2.  SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
2.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu was built by the extrusion of basaltic lava from the Waianae 
and Koolau shield volcanoes. The older Waianae Volcano is estimated to be middle to 
late Pliocene in age and the younger Koolau Volcano is estimated to be late Pliocene to 
early Pleistocene in age.  After a long period of volcanic inactivity, during which time 
erosion incised deep valleys into the Koolau Shield, volcanic activity returned with a 
series of lava flows followed by cinder and tuff cone eruptions.  These later eruptions 
are referred to as the Honolulu Volcanic Series. 
During the Pleistocene Epoch (Ice Age), sea levels fluctuated in response to the 
cycles of continental glaciation.  As the glaciers grew and advanced, less water was 
available to fill the oceanic basins such that sea levels fell below the present stands of 
the sea.  When the glaciers melted and receded, an excess of water became available 
such that the sea levels rose to above the present sea level. 
The processes of erosion and deposition were affected by these glacio-eustatic 
sea level fluctuations.  When the sea level was low, the erosional base level was 
correspondingly lower, and valleys were carved to depths below the present sea level.  
When the sea level was high, the erosional base level was raised such that sediments 
accumulated at higher elevations. 
In the mountainous regions of Hawaii, the erosion processes are dominated by 
detachment of soil and rock masses from the valley walls and are transported 
downslope toward the axis of a valley primarily by gravity as colluvium. Once these 
materials reach the stream in the central portion of a valley, alluvial processes become 
dominant, and the sediments are transported and deposited as alluvium. 
The project site is located within the central valley floor. Basalt rock formation of 
the Koolau Volcanic Series forms the stratigraphic base of the region and is believed to 
underlie the near-surface deposits of mixed alluvial and colluvial materials.

2.2 
Visual Pavement Condition Survey 
A pavement condition survey of the project area was performed by our engineer. 
During the field survey, our engineer walked on the sides of the street and determined 
pavement distress types through visual classification. 
The visual classification was performed in general accordance with the 
definitions described in the “Distress Identification Manual for the Long-Term Pavement 
Performance Program,” dated June 2003, prepared by the Federal Highway 
Administration (FHWA). Some of the pavement distress definitions are presented in the 
table below. 
ASPHALT PAVEMENT DISTRESSES  
Alligator Cracking 
(Fatigue) 
Alligator cracks are a series of interconnecting cracks caused by 
fatigue failure of the asphaltic concrete surface under repeated 
traffic loading. 
Block Cracking 
Block cracks are inter-connected cracks that divide the pavement 
into approximately rectangular pieces. 
Longitudinal 
Cracking 
Longitudinal cracks are cracks parallel to the pavement’s 
centerline or laydown direction. 
Transverse 
Cracking 
Transverse cracks are cracks extending across the pavement at 
approximately right angles to the pavement centerline or direction 
of laydown. 
Patch 
Patch is an area of pavement that has been replaced with new 
material to repair the existing pavement. 
Pothole 
Potholes are small bowl-shaped (usually less than 30 inches in 
diameter) depressions in the pavement surface. 
Rutting 
A rut is a surface depression in the wheel paths. 
Shoving 
Shoving is a longitudinal displacement of a localized area of the 
pavement surface generally caused by braking or accelerating of 
vehicles.  Shoving is usually located on hills or curves, or at 
intersections.  It also may have associated vertical displacement. 
Bleeding 
Excess bituminous binder occurring on the pavement surface, 
usually found in the wheel paths. 
Polished 
Aggregate 
Polished Aggregate is the area of pavement where the portion of 
aggregate extending above the asphalt binder is either very small, 
or there are no rough or angular aggregate particles to provide 
good skid resistance. 
Weathering & 
Raveling 
Weathering and raveling are wearing away of the pavement 
surface due to a loss of asphalt or tar binder and dislodged 
aggregate particles.

2.3 
Subsurface Conditions 
Our field exploration program consisted of drilling and sampling 24 borings, 
designated as Boring Nos. 1 through 24, at selected locations along the roadways. The 
borings generally extended to depths of about 1 to 7 feet below the existing pavement 
surface. In addition, the boring exploration was supplemented by coring the existing 
pavement section to depths of about 2 to 3 feet below the existing pavement surface at 
34 selected locations, identified as Coring Nos. 101 through 134. The approximate 
boring and coring locations for this project are shown on the Site Plans, Plates 3.1 
through 3.27. 
In addition, DCP testing using a Dynamic Cone Penetrometer was conducted in 
Boring Nos. 2 through 24 to estimate the CBR value of the subgrade soils. This 
instrument is typically used to assess material properties of the pavement subgrade 
soils. The 17.6-pound DCP can be used to estimate the strength characteristics of fine 
and coarse-grained soils, granular construction materials and weak stabilized or 
modified materials. The 17.6 pound DCP cannot be used in highly stabilized or 
cemented materials or for granular materials containing a large percentage of 
aggregates greater than 2 inches. The results of the DCP testing are shown on the DCP 
logs in Appendix C. 
Based on our field exploration and laboratory testing program, the existing 
pavement structure encountered and the CBR values estimated from the DCP testing 
conducted along University Avenue, Manoa Road and East Manoa Road are 
summarized below:

The pavement structure at University Avenue consisted of about 4 to 9 inches of 
AC underlain by base/subbase material about 3.5 to 12.5 inches thick. Coring Nos. 105 
and 107 did not encounter base/subbase material below the AC. On average, the 
pavement structure generally consists of about 6.5 inches of AC over 5 inches of 
base/subbase material.  The subgrade soils generally consisted of dense sand and 
gravel and stiff clayey soils.  Soft to medium hard basalt rock was encountered near 
Oahu Avenue. 
Our field exploration at Manoa Road generally encountered a pavement structure 
that consisted of 3.75 to 8 inches of AC.  Portland Cement Concrete (PCC) about 6 to 
8 inches thick was encountered in Boring Nos. 5 and 6 and Coring Nos. 108 and 109.  
Below the AC and PCC, the borings and corings encountered about 1 to 18.25 inches of 
base/subbase material.  Boring No. 6 and Coring No. 108 did not encounter 
base/subbase materials.  The average thicknesses of the AC and PCC are about 5 and 
SECTION 2. SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
W.O. 6947-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 9 
Hawaii • California 
7 inches, respectively, and the average thickness of the base/subbase material is about 
7.5 inches.  The subgrade soils generally consisted of stiff clayey soils.  
The pavement structure at East Manoa Road generally consisted of 4 to 
11 inches of AC. Portland Cement Concrete (PCC) about 3 to 5.25 inches thick was 
encountered in Boring Nos. 17 through 19 and Coring Nos. 124 through 126.   Below 
the AC and PCC, the borings and corings encountered about 3.5 to 15.5 inches of 
base/subbase material.  Boring No. 24 and Coring Nos. 124, 133, and 134 did not 
encounter base/subbase materials.  Clayey soils were generally encountered below the 
base/subbase material.  
We did not encounter groundwater in our shallow borings (maximum depth of 
7.0 feet below the pavement surface) at the time of our field exploration. However, it 
should be noted that groundwater levels are expected to fluctuate depending on 
seasonal rainfall, time of year, surface runoff, and other factors.  
Detailed descriptions of the materials encountered in the borings and corings are

calculations are presented in Appendix D. Photographs of the AC cores are provided in 
Appendix E. Photographs depicting some of the noted distresses along the roadways 
are presented in Appendix F.

SECTION 3.  DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS 
We believe that the poor bonding of the AC overlays/lifts may have attributed to 
the distresses that developed in these roadways.  Our borings also indicated that the 
AC surface course might be underlain by PCC pavements at some areas of Manoa 
Road and East Manoa Road.  Existing cracks in the PCC could have reflected up to the 
AC surface course.  
Based on our observation of the existing pavements, we recommend 
reconstruction of these roadways, which consist of removing the existing AC, PCC and 
portions of the existing base/subbase, if required, and replacing with a new pavement 
section consisting of asphaltic concrete, asphalt concrete base, and aggregate 
subbase.  The recommended pavement sections are provided in the “New Pavement 
Design” section of this report. 
If soft soils are exposed at the pavement subgrade level, we recommend 
providing a stabilization layer in lieu of proof-rolling the soft subgrade.  The stabilization 
layer should consist of 8 inches of aggregate base course over a layer of geogrid (such 
as Tensar Triax TX7 or equivalent) underlain by a woven geotextile fabric (such as 
Mirafi HP370 or equivalent).    
Ideally, we recommend reconstructing the entire roadways with the new 
pavement sections.  However, due to potential budget constraints, the existing 
base/subbase layer may be utilized, where possible, in the new pavement sections. If 
these pavement sections utilizing the existing base/subbase layer are adopted, it should 
be made aware that there exists the potential for localized areas to experience 
premature pavement distress and/or failure depending on the actual thickness and 
quality of the remaining existing base/subbase layer. 
The following sections provide discussion on the areas necessary for pavement 
analyses and design. 
Methodology of Pavement Design 
Design Traffic Loading Conditions

3.3 
Design Subgrade Conditions 
Laboratory CBR tests (ASTM D1883) were performed on 14 bulk samples, which 
consisted of soil cuttings of the pavement subgrade soils, to evaluate the swelling 
potential and pavement support characteristics of the pavement subgrade soils.  The 
bulk samples were designated as Bulk-1 (at B-2), Bulk-2 (at B-5), Bulk-3 (at B-7), Bulk-4 
(at B-8), Bulk-5 (at B-10), Bulk-6 (at B-12), Bulk-7 (at B-14), Bulk-8 (at B-15), Bulk-9 (at 
B-16), Bulk-10 (at B-18), Bulk-11 (at B-20), Bulk-12 (at B-21), Bulk-13 (at B-23), and 
Bulk-14 (at B-24).  
Full closure of these roadways during pavement reconstruction is not practical.  It 
is likely that the roadways will be closed for about 6 to 8 hours each day.  Due to the 
limited closure time for the pavement reconstruction work, it may not be practical to 
scarify and moisture-condition the pavement subgrade soils.  Since it is not practical to 
scarify and moisture-condition the pavement subgrade soils, the CBR tests were 
conducted on bulk soil samples that were remolded at the in-situ moisture to simulate 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 6947-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 15 
Hawaii • California 
the condition that the subgrade soils will be compacted at the in-situ moisture content.

3.4 
New Pavement Design 
Our field exploration indicated that there was poor bonding of the existing AC 
overlays/lifts at various locations along the roadways.  We believe that the poor bonding 
of the AC overlays/lifts may have attributed to the distresses that developed in the 
pavements.  In addition, our field exploration results indicate that the AC surface course 
may be underlain by PCC pavements along some areas of Manoa Road and East 
Manoa Road.  Leaving the existing PCC pavement and placing new AC over the PCC 
may result in existing cracks in the PCC pavement reflecting up to the new AC surface. 
Based on the information provided, we recommend pavement reconstruction at 
University Avenue, Manoa Road, and East Manoa Road.  Reconstruction would consist 
of removing the existing AC and existing base/subbase, if required, and replacing with a 
new pavement section consisting of asphaltic concrete, asphalt concrete base, and 
aggregate subbase course. Detailed analyses and calculations for the pavement design 
are presented on Plates D-11 through D-15 in Appendix D.  Based on the above 
assumptions, we recommend utilizing the following flexible pavement sections for 
design.   
University Avenue (Coyne Street to Dole Street), Manoa Road (Nehoa Street to 
East Manoa Road), and East Manoa Road 
3.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete  
4.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete Base (92 Percent Max. Theoretical Specific Gravity) 
 6.0-Inch Aggregate Subbase Course (95 Percent Relative Compaction) 
 13.0-Inch Total New Pavement Thickness 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 6947-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 17 
Hawaii • California 
University Avenue (Dole Street to Oahu Avenue) 
2.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete  
4.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete Base (92 Percent Max. Theoretical Specific Gravity) 
 6.0-Inch Aggregate Subbase Course (95 Percent Relative Compaction) 
 12.0-Inch Total New Pavement Thickness 
Manoa Road (East Manoa Road to Mauka End) 
2.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete  
3.0-Inch Asphaltic Concrete Base (92 Percent Max. Theoretical Specific Gravity) 
 6.0-Inch Aggregate Subbase Course (95 Percent Relative Compaction) 
 11.0-Inch Total New Pavement Thickness 
3.5 
Alternative Pavement Sections 
Due to potential budget constraints, the existing base/subbase layer may be 
utilized, where possible, in the new pavement sections. If these alternative pavement 
sections are adopted, it should be made aware that there exists the potential for 
localized areas to experience premature pavement distress and/or failure depending on 
the actual thickness and quality of the remaining existing base/subbase layer. 
For University Avenue, between Coyne and Dole Streets and between Dole 
Street and Maile Way, alternative pavement sections consisting of 3 inches of AC over 
4 inches of Asphalt Concrete Base (ACB) and 2 inches of AC over 4 inches of ACB, 
respectively, may be considered by taking into account the contribution of the remaining 
existing base/subbase layer.  Based on our borings, a base/subbase layer was not 
encountered below the existing AC along University Avenue between Maile Way and 
Oahu Avenue. 
For Manoa Road, between East Manoa Road and the Mauka end, an alternative 
pavement section consisting of 2 inches of AC over 3 inches of ACB may be considered 
for this section by taking into account the contribution of the remaining existing 
base/subbase layer.   
For East Manoa Road up to the intersection of Kinohou Place, an alternative 
pavement section consisting of 3 inches of AC over 4 inches of ACB may be considered 
for this section by taking into account the contribution of the remaining existing 
base/subbase layer.  Based on our borings, a base/subbase layer was not encountered 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 6947-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 18 
Hawaii • California 
below the existing AC along East Manoa Road north of the intersection of Kinohou 
Place. 
3.6 
Site Preparation 
After removal of the existing AC and base/subbase layers to accommodate the 
new pavement structural sections, the exposed subgrade soils should be proof-rolled 
with a minimum 10-ton (static weight) drum roller for a minimum of eight passes to 
compact the exposed subgrade soils. The drum roller should be operated at a speed of 
about 300 feet per minute.  
If subgrade soils with relatively high in-situ moisture are exposed, we recommend 
proof-rolling the pavement subgrade without the use of vibration. Use of vibration during 
proof-rolling will induce a pumping condition of the subgrade soils with high in-situ 
moisture. 
If soft soils are exposed at the pavement subgrade level, we recommend 
providing a stabilization layer in lieu of proof-rolling the soft subgrade, which may induce 
pumping condition.  The stabilization layer should consist of 8 inches of aggregate base 
course over a layer of geogrid (such as Tensar Triax TX7 or equivalent) underlain by a 
woven geotextile fabric (such as Mirafi HP370 or equivalent) as shown on Plate 5. The 
aggregate base course should be moisture-conditioned to above the optimum moisture 
content and compacted to a minimum of 95 percent relative compaction. Relative 
compaction refers to the in-place dry density of soil expressed as a percentage of the 
maximum dry density of the same soil established in accordance with AASHTO T-180 
(ASTM D1557). Optimum moisture is the water content (percentage by dry weight) 
corresponding to the maximum dry density. 
A Geolabs representative should monitor the pavement subgrade preparation to 
observe whether undesirable materials are encountered during the excavation and 
scarification process and to confirm whether the exposed soil/rock conditions are similar 
to those encountered in our field exploration. 
Asphalt concrete base (ACB) material should consist of asphalt-treated basalt 
aggregate, placed in a layer not to exceed 6 inches in compacted thickness, and 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 6947-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 19 
Hawaii • California 
compacted to no less than 92 percent of the maximum theoretical specific gravity 
determined in accordance with AASHTO T-209 or ASTM D2041. Asphalt concrete (AC) 
material should be constructed in accordance with Section 401, Asphalt Concrete 
Pavement of the Hawaii Standard Specifications for Road and Bridge Construction, and 
its amendments. 
3.7 
Surface Drainage 
One of the primary distress mechanisms in pavement structures is pumping due 
to saturation of the subbase and base course and/or subgrade soils. Therefore, the 
pavement surface should be sloped to drain and drainage gradients should be 
maintained to carry surface water off the pavement to appropriate drainage structures. 
Surface water ponding should not be allowed during or after construction. Development 
of good shoulder drainage along with a program to prevent obstructions in the drainage 
structures is strongly recommended to reduce the potential for pavement deterioration 
or premature failure of the pavements. 
3.8 
Design Review 
Drawings and specifications for the project should be forwarded to Geolabs for 
review and written comments prior to bid advertisement. This review is necessary to 
evaluate conformance of the plans and specifications to the intent of the pavement and 
earthwork recommendations provided herein. If this review is not made, Geolabs cannot 
be responsible for misinterpretation of the recommendations presented. 
3.9 
Construction Monitoring 
Geolabs should be retained to provide geotechnical engineering services during 
construction. The critical items of construction monitoring include observation of the 
excavation of the pavement reconstruction areas and subgrade preparation. This is to 
observe compliance with the design concepts, specifications, or recommendations and 
to expedite suggestions for design changes that may be required in the event that 
subsurface conditions differ from those anticipated at the time this report was prepared. 
The recommendations provided herein are contingent upon such observations. If the 
actual exposed subsurface conditions encountered during construction are different 
SECTION 3. DISCUSSION AND RECOMMENDATIONS  
W.O. 6947-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 20 
Hawaii • California 
from those assumed or considered in this report, then appropriate design modifications 
should be made.

SECTION 4.  LIMITATIONS 
The analyses and recommendations submitted herein are based in part upon 
information obtained from the field borings, field corings, DCP testing, and bulk 
samples. Variations of subsurface conditions between and beyond the field data points 
may occur, and the nature and extent of these variations may not become evident until 
construction is underway. If variations then appear evident, Geolabs should be 
contacted to re-evaluate the recommendations presented herein. 
The field exploration locations are approximate, having been estimated by pacing 
and measuring from visible features shown on Google EarthTM. The boring and coring 
locations should be considered accurate only to the degree implied by the methods 
used. 
The stratification lines shown on the graphic representations of the borings and 
corings depict the approximate boundaries between soil/rock types and, as such, may 
denote a gradual transition. Water level data from the borings and corings were 
measured at the times shown on the graphic representations and/or presented in the 
text herein. These data have been reviewed and interpretations made to formulate this 
report. However, it must be noted that fluctuation may occur due to seasonal 
precipitation, surface water runoff, and other factors. 
This report has been prepared for the exclusive use of AECOM, their client, City 
and County of Honolulu - Department of Design and Construction, and other project 
subconsultants for specific application to the Rehabilitation of City Streets, Unit 81 
Portions of University Avenue, Manoa Road and East Manoa Road project in general 
accordance with geotechnical engineering principles and practices. No warranty is 
expressed or implied. 
This report has been prepared solely for the purpose of assisting the design 
engineer in the preparation of design drawings related to the pavements for the 
proposed project only. Therefore, this report may not contain sufficient data, or the 
proper information, to serve as a basis for construction cost estimates. A contractor 
wishing to bid on this project is urged to retain a competent geotechnical engineer to 
SECTION 4. LIMITATIONS 
W.O. 6947-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 22 
Hawaii • California 
assist in the interpretation of this report and/or in the performance of additional 
site-specific exploration for bid estimating purposes. 
The owner/client should be aware that unanticipated soil and/or rock conditions 
are commonly encountered. Unforeseen subsurface conditions, such as soft deposits, 
hard layers, cavities, or perched groundwater, may occur in localized areas and may 
require additional probing or corrections in the field (which may result in construction 
delays) to attain a properly constructed project. Therefore, a sufficient contingency fund 
is recommended to accommodate these possible extra costs. 
This geotechnical engineering exploration conducted at the project site was not 
intended to investigate the potential presence of hazardous materials existing at the 
site. It should be noted that the equipment, techniques, and personnel used to conduct 
a geo-environmental exploration differ substantially from those applied in geotechnical 
engineering. 
END OF LIMITATIONS

APPENDIX  A

A P P E N D I X   A 
Field Exploration – Borings and Corings 
We explored the subsurface conditions along the roadway rehabilitation project by 
drilling and sampling 24 borings, designated as Boring Nos. 1 through 24, (Plates A-1 
through A-24) extending to depths ranging from about 1 to 7 feet below the existing 
pavement surface. In addition, the boring exploration was supplemented by coring the 
existing pavement section to depths of about 2 to 3 feet below the existing pavement 
surface at 34 selected locations, identified as Coring Nos. 101 through 134 (Plates A-25 
through A-58). The approximate boring and coring locations for this project are shown on 
the Site Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.27. The borings were drilled using a truck-mounted 
drill rig equipped with continuous flight augers and coring tools. 
Our engineer and geologist classified the materials encountered in the borings and 
corings by visual and textural examination in the field in general accordance with 
ASTM D2488, Standard Practice for Description and Identification of Soils, and monitored 
the drilling operations on a near-continuous (full-time) basis. These classifications were 
further reviewed visually and by testing in the laboratory. Soils were classified in general 
accordance with ASTM D2487, Standard Practice for Classification of Soils for 
Engineering Purposes (Unified Soil Classification System), as shown on the Soil Log 
Legend, Plate A-0.1. Deviations made to the soil classification in accordance with 
ASTM D2487 are described on the Soil Classification Log Key, Plate A-0.2. Graphic 
representations of the materials encountered are presented on the Logs of Borings and 
Corings, Plates A-1 through A-58. 
Relatively “undisturbed” soil samples were obtained in general accordance with 
ASTM D3550, Ring-Lined Barrel Sampling of Soils, by driving a 3-inch OD Modified 
California sampler with a 140-pound hammer falling 30 inches. In addition, some samples 
were obtained from the drilled borings and corings in general accordance with 
ASTM D1586, Penetration Test and Split-Barrel Sampling of Soils, by driving a 2-inch OD 
standard penetration sampler using the same hammer and drop. The blow counts needed 
to drive the sampler the second and third 6 inches of an 18-inch drive are shown as the 
“Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Borings and Corings at the appropriate sample 
depths. The penetration resistance shown on the logs of borings indicates the number of 
blows required for the specific sampler type used. The blow counts may need to be 
factored to obtain the Standard Penetration Test (SPT) blow counts. 
Pocket penetrometer tests were performed on selected cohesive soil samples in 
the field. The pocket penetrometer test provides an indication of the unconfined 
compressive strength of the sample. The pocket penetrometer test results are summarized 
on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. 
Core samples of the rock materials encountered at the project site were obtained 
by using diamond core drilling techniques in general accordance with ASTM D2113, 
Diamond Core Drilling for Site Investigation. Core drilling is a rotary drilling method that 
Appendix A 
Field Exploration 
W.O. 6947-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MAY 2019     Page A-2  
Hawaii • California 
uses a hollow bit to cut into the rock formation. The rock material left in the hollow core of 
the bit is mechanically recovered for examination and description. Rock cores were 
described in general accordance with the Rock Description System as shown on the Rock 
Log Legend, Plate A-0.3.   
The Rock Description System is based on the publication “Suggested Methods for 
the Quantitative Description of Discontinuities in Rock Masses” by the International Society 
for Rock Mechanics (March 1977). 
Recovery (REC) is used as a subjective guide to the interpretation of the relative 
quality of rock masses. Recovery is defined as the actual length of material recovered 
from a coring attempt versus the length of the core attempt. For example, if 3.7 feet of 
material is recovered from a 5.0-foot core run, the recovery would be 74 percent and 
would be shown on the Logs of Borings as REC = 74%. 
The Rock Quality Designation (RQD) is also a subjective guide to the relative 
quality of rock masses. RQD is defined as the percentage of the core run in rock that is 
sound material in excess of 4 inches in length without any discontinuities, discounting 
any drilling induced fractures or breaks. If 2.5 feet of sound material is recovered from a 
5.0-foot core run in rock, the RQD would be 50 percent and would be shown on the 
Logs of Borings as RQD = 50%. Generally, the following is used to describe the relative 
quality of the rock based on the "Practical Handbook of Physical Properties of Rocks 
and Minerals." 
Rock Quality 
RQD 
(%) 
Very Poor 
0 – 25 
Poor 
25 – 50 
Fair 
50 – 75 
Good 
75 – 90 
Excellent 
90 – 100 
The rippability (or excavation characteristics) of a rock mass is a function of the 
relative hardness of the rock, its relative quality, brittleness, and fissile characteristics. 
Dense basalt formations with high RQD values would be very difficult to rip and would 
probably require more arduous methods of excavation.

PRIMARY constituents are composed of the largest
percent of the soil mass. Primary constituents are
capitalized and bold (i.e., GRAVEL, SAND)
PRIMARY constituents are based on plasticity. Primary
constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e., CLAY, SILT)
SECONDARY constituents are composed of a
percentage less than the primary constituent. If the soil
mass consists of 12 percent or more fines content, a
cohesive constituent is used (SILTY or CLAYEY);
otherwise, a granular constituent is used (GRAVELLY
or SANDY) provided that the secondary constituent
consists of 20 percent or more of the soil mass.
Secondary constituents are capitalized and bold (i.e.,
SANDY GRAVEL, CLAYEY SAND) and precede the
primary constituent.
SECONDARY constituents are composed of a
percentage less than the primary constituent, but more
than 20 percent of the soil mass. Secondary constituents
are capitalized and bold (i.e., SANDY CLAY, SILTY
CLAY, CLAYEY SILT) and precede the primary
constituent.

Rock Log Legend
The following terms describe the resistance of a rock to indentation or scratching:
Specimen breaks with difficulty after several "pinging" hammer blows.
Example: Dense, fine grain volcanic rock
Specimen breaks with some difficulty after several hammer blows.
Example: Vesicular, vugular, coarse-grained rock
Specimen can be broked by one hammer blow. Cannot be scraped by knife. SPT may penetrate by
~25 blows per inch with bounce.
Example: Porous rock such as clinker, cinder, and coral reef
Can be indented by one hammer blow. Can be scraped or peeled by knife. SPT can penetrate by
~100 blows per foot.
Example: Weathered rock, chalk-like coral reef
Crumbles under hammer blow. Can be peeled and carved by knife. Can be indented by finger
pressure.
Example: Saprolite
CONGLOMERATE

APPENDIX  B  
W.O. 6947-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MAY 2019     Page B-1 
Hawaii • California 
A P P E N D I X   B 
Laboratory Tests 
Moisture Content (ASTM D2216) and Unit Weight (ASTM D2937) determinations 
were performed on selected samples as an aid in the classification and evaluation of 
soil properties. The test results are presented on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate 
sample depths. 
Thirteen Atterberg Limits tests (ASTM D4318) were performed on selected soil 
samples to evaluate the liquid and plastic limits. The test results are summarized on the 
Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. Graphic presentations of the test 
results are provided on Plates B-1 and B-2. 
Four Sieve Analysis tests (ASTM C117 & C136) were performed on selected soil 
samples to evaluate the gradation characteristics of the soils and to aid in soil 
classification.  Graphic presentations of the grain size distribution are provided on 
Plate B-3. 
Fourteen laboratory California Bearing Ratio tests (ASTM D1883) were 
performed on bulk samples of the subgrade soils to evaluate the pavement support 
characteristics of the soils. The test results are presented on Plates B-4 through B-17.

APPENDIX  C  
W.O. 6947-00 
GEOLABS, INC. 
MAY 2019     Page C-1 
Hawaii • California 
A P P E N D I X   C 
Dynamic Cone Penetration Tests 
Dynamic Cone Penetration (DCP) testing was performed at the 23 boring 
locations (Boring Nos. 2 through 24) to obtain pavement support characteristics of the 
in-situ soils. The tests were performed using a Salem Dual Mass Dynamic Cone 
Penetrometer to a depth of about 1.5 to 5 feet below the existing pavement surface. 
The DCP device consists of two 16mm-diameter stainless steel rods with the 
lower rod containing an anvil, a replaceable 60o pointed tip and depth markings. The 
upper rod holds a 17.6-pound drop hammer with a 22.6-inch drop distance. At the 
beginning of a test, the cone is “seated” by dropping the hammer until the widest part of 
the cone is just below the testing surface at which point the initial penetration is 
recorded as 0. The operator then lifts and drops the hammer one or more times 
depending on the strength of the soil at the test location. The number of blows for a 
minimum of 1-inch penetration is recorded.  The process is continued until the desired 
depth of testing is reached or the full length of the lower rod is buried. 
Data from our DCP testing indicates the penetration index (PI) which is simply the 
penetration distance of the cone for each drop of the hammer expressed as millimeters 
per blow. The penetration indices obtained from our tests were correlated with common 
pavement design parameters such as California Bearing Ratio CBR. Graphic 
representations of the materials encountered are presented on the DCP Logs, Plates C-1 
through C-23.2.
